We consider the Online Convex Optimization (OCO) problem over a convex set ūĚí¶‚äā‚ĄĚdfragmentsKRūĚĎĎ\mathcal{K}\subset\mathbb{R}^{d}, in which a learner (algorithm) plays a game against an adaptive adversary for TūĚĎáT rounds. At each round t‚ąą[T]fragmentst[T]t\in[T], the learner picks wt‚ąąūĚí¶fragmentswūĚĎ°Kw_{t}\in\mathcal{K} given knowledge of the history ‚Ąčt‚ąí1‚ČĒ{(‚Ąďs,ws)}s<tfragmentsHfragmentst1‚ČĒ{(‚ĄďūĚĎ†,wūĚĎ†)}fragmentsst\mathcal{H}_{t-1}\coloneqq\{(\ell_{s},w_{s})\}_{s<t}. Then, the adversary picks a convex loss function ‚Ąďt:ūĚí¶‚Üí‚ĄĚfragments‚ĄďūĚĎ°:K‚ÜíR\ell_{t}:\mathcal{K}\rightarrow\mathbb{R} with the knowledge of ‚Ąčt‚ąí1fragmentsHfragmentst1\mathcal{H}_{t-1} and the iterate wtfragmentswūĚĎ°w_{t}, and the learner suffers loss ‚Ąďt(wt)fragments‚ĄďūĚĎ°(wūĚĎ°)\ell_{t}(w_{t}) and proceeds to the next round. The goal of the learner is to minimize the regret after TūĚĎáT rounds:RegT(w)=‚ąĎt=1T‚Ąďt(wt)‚ąí‚ąĎt=1T‚Ąďt(w),fragmentsRegūĚĎá(w)fragmentst1ūĚĎá‚ĄďūĚĎ°(wūĚĎ°)fragmentst1ūĚĎá‚ĄďūĚĎ°(w),\displaystyle\mathrm{Reg}_{T}(w)=\sum_{t=1}^{T}\ell_{t}(w_{t})-\sum_{t=1}^{T}\ell_{t}(w),against any comparator w‚ąąūĚí¶fragmentswKw\in\mathcal{K}. The aim of this paper is to design computationally-efficient (projection-free) algorithms for OCO that enjoy the optimal (up to log-factor in TūĚĎáT) O~(T)fragments~ūĚĎā(ūĚĎá)\widetilde{O}(\sqrt{T}) regret.

The OCO framework captures many optimization settings relevant to machine learning applications. For example, OCO algorithms can be used in offline convex optimization as more computationally- and memory-efficient alternatives to interior-point and cutting plane methods whenever the dimension dūĚĎĎd is large [HK12, Jag13]. OCO algorithms are also often used in stochastic convex optimization, where the standard O(T)fragmentsO(ūĚĎá)O(\sqrt{T}) regret (achieved by, e.g.¬†Online Gradient Descent) translates into the optimal O(1/T)fragmentsO(1ūĚĎá)O(1/\sqrt{T}) rate111This is the optimal rate when no further assumptions are made. via the classical online-to-batch conversion technique [CBCG04, SS+{}^{+}11]. It has been shown that OCO algorithms can also achieve state-of-the-art accelerated rates in both the offline and stochastic optimization settings despite being designed for the more general OCO framework [Cut19, Mha22a]. What is more, it has recently been shown that even non-convex (stochastic) optimization can be reduced to online linear optimization (a special case of OCO), where it is then possible to recover the best-known convergence rates for the setting [CMO23].

Given the prevalent use of OCO algorithms in machine learning applications, it is important to have computationally-efficient algorithms that scale well with the dimension dūĚĎĎd of the ambient space. However, most OCO algorithms fall short of being efficient because of the need of performing (Euclidean) projections onto ūĚí¶ūĚí¶\mathcal{K} (potentially at each iteration) to ensure that the iterates are feasible. These projections are often inefficient, especially in high-dimensional settings with complex feasible sets. Existing projection-free OCO algorithms address this computational challenge by swapping potentially-expensive Euclidean projections for often much cheaper linear optimization or separation over the feasible set ūĚí¶ūĚí¶\mathcal{K}. However, existing projection-free algorithms have sub-optimal regret guarantees in terms of their dependence in TūĚĎáT, or have potentially unbounded ‚Äúcondition numbers‚ÄĚ for the feasible set multiplying their regret guarantee.

In this paper, we address these computational and performance challenges by revisiting an existing (but somewhat overlooked) type of projection-free OCO algorithms. Unlike existing algorithms, our proposed method does not require linear optimization or separation over the feasible set ūĚí¶ūĚí¶\mathcal{K}. Instead, the algorithm, Barrier-Regularized Online Newton Step (BARONS),222We credit the name BARONS to [LWZ18] who used barrier-regularized Newton steps for the portfolio selection problem. uses a self-concordant barrier ő¶ő¶\Phi for the set ūĚí¶ūĚí¶\mathcal{K} to always output iterates that are guaranteed to be within ūĚí¶ūĚí¶\mathcal{K}; much like interior point methods for offline optimization. In particular, our algorithm outputs Newton iterates with respect to time-varying, translated versions of ő¶ő¶\Phi. The main novelty of our work is in devising a new efficient way of computing the Newton iterates without having to evaluate the inverse of the Hessian of the barrier at every iteration, which can be computationally expensive in high-dimensional settings. Our algorithm only needs to compute a full inverse of the Hessian a vanishing O(1/T)fragmentsO(1ūĚĎá)O(1/\sqrt{T}) fraction of the rounds. For the rest of the rounds, the computational cost is dominated by that of evaluating the gradient of the barrier ő¶ő¶\Phi, which can be much cheaper than evaluating the inverse of its Hessian in many cases.

For the special case of a polytope with mūĚĎöm constraints, we show that there is a choice of a barrier (e.g.¬†the Lee-Sidford barrier) that when used within our algorithm, reduces the per-round computational cost to essentially O~(1)fragments~ūĚĎā(1)\widetilde{O}(1) linear-system-solves of size m√ódfragmentsmdm\times d. We show that this is often cheaper than performing linear optimization over ūĚí¶ūĚí¶\mathcal{K}, which other projection-free algorithms require. More importantly, our algorithm achieves a dimension-free O~(T)fragments~ūĚĎā(ūĚĎá)\widetilde{O}(\sqrt{T}) regret bound. This improves over the existing regret bounds of projection-free algorithms over polytopes. For example, among projection-free algorithms that achieve a O(T)fragmentsO(ūĚĎá)O(\sqrt{T}) regret, the algorithms by [Mha22a, GK22, LBGH23], which require a separation/membership Oracle for ūĚí¶ūĚí¶\mathcal{K}, have a multiplicative őļ=R/rfragmentsőļRr\kappa=R/r factor multiplying their regret bounds, where r,R>0fragmentsr,R0r,R>0 are such that ‚Ą¨(r)‚äÜūĚí¶‚äÜ‚Ą¨(R)fragmentsB(r)KB(R)\mathcal{B}(r)\subseteq\mathcal{K}\subseteq\mathcal{B}(R). The constant őļūĚúÖ\kappa, known as the asphercity [Gof88], can in principle be arbitrarily large. Even after applying a potentially expensive pre-processing step, which would typically involve putting the set ūĚí¶ūĚí¶\mathcal{K} into (near-) isotropic position [FKM05, Tko18], őļūĚúÖ\kappa can still be as large as dūĚĎĎd in the worst-case, and so the regret bounds achieved by the algorithms of [Mha22a, GK22, LBGH23] can be of order O(dT)fragmentsO(dūĚĎá)O(d\sqrt{T}); this is worse than ours by a dūĚĎĎd factor. Other projection-free algorithms based on the Frank-Wolfe method, e.g.¬†those in [GH16, PNAJ20, LJJ15], also have multiplicative condition numbers that are even less benign that the asphercity őļūĚúÖ\kappa. In fact, the condition numbers in the regret bounds for polytopes appearing in, e.g.¬†[GH16], can in principle be arbitrarily large regardless of any pre-processing.

Finally, another advantage of our algorithm is that it can guarantee a sublinear regret even for non-Lipschitz losses (i.e.¬†where the norm of the sub-gradients may be unbounded). In particular, we show that the general guarantee of BARONS implies a O~(dT)fragments~ūĚĎā(fragmentsdT)\widetilde{O}(\sqrt{dT}) regret bound for the portfolio selection problem [Cov91] and a problem of linear prediction with log-loss [RS15], all while keeping the per-round computational cost under O~(d2)fragments~ūĚĎā(d2)\widetilde{O}(d^{2}), when T‚Č•dfragmentsTdT\geq d. The losses in both of these problems are neither bounded or Lispchitz.

In the past decade, many projection-free OCO algorithms have been developed to address the computational shortcoming of their projection-based counter parts [HK12, Jag13, HM20, KG21, Mha22a, GK22]. Most projection-free algorithms are based on the Frank-Wolfe method and perform linear optimization (typically once per round) over ūĚí¶ūĚí¶\mathcal{K} instead of Euclidean projection. Under no additional assumptions other than convexity and lipschitzness of the losses, the best-known regret bound for such algorithms scales as O(T3/4)fragmentsO(Tfragments34)O(T^{3/4}) [HM20]. While this bound is still sublinear in TūĚĎáT and has no dependence in the dimension dūĚĎĎd, it is sub-optimal compared to the O(T)fragmentsO(ūĚĎá)O(\sqrt{T}) regret bound achievable with projection-based algorithms. In the recent years, there have been improvements to this bound under additional assumptions such as when the functions are smooth and/or strongly convex [HM20, KG21], or when the convex set ūĚí¶ūĚí¶\mathcal{K} is smooth and/or strongly convex [ALLW18, LK19, Mha22b, LG23]. For the case where ūĚí¶ūĚí¶\mathcal{K} is a polytope, [GH16] presented a linear-optimization-based algorithm that enjoys a O(őľdT)fragmentsO(őľfragmentsdT)O(\mu\sqrt{dT}) regret bound, where őľūĚúá\mu is a conditioning number for the set ūĚí¶ūĚí¶\mathcal{K}. Unfortunately, őľūĚúá\mu can be large for many sets of interests as it essentially scales inversely with the minimum distance between the vertices of ūĚí¶ūĚí¶\mathcal{K}. In this work, we achieve a dimension-free O~(T)fragments~ūĚĎā(ūĚĎá)\widetilde{O}(\sqrt{T}) regret bound without the őľūĚúá\mu factor.

More recently a new type of projection-free algorithms have emerged which use membership/separation oracle calls instead of linear optimization [Mha22a, GK22, LG23, LBGH23]. From a computational perspective, separation-based and linear optimization-based algorithms are not really comparable, since there are sets over which separation is cheaper than linear optimization, and vice-versa. On the regret side, separation-based algorithms have been show to achieve a O(őļT)fragmentsO(őļūĚĎá)O(\kappa\sqrt{T}) regret bound, where őļūĚúÖ\kappa is the asphercity of the set ūĚí¶ūĚí¶\mathcal{K}. Separation-based algorithms are simple, often easy to analyze, and achieve the optimal-in-TūĚĎáT regret bound, unlike linear optimization-based algorithms. However, the multiplicative factor őļūĚúÖ\kappa in their regret bounds means that a pre-conditioning step may be required to ensure it is appropriately bounded. This precondition step would involve putting the set into (near-) isotropic position [FKM05]; an operation, that can cost O~(d4)fragments~ūĚĎā(d4)\widetilde{O}(d^{4}) arithmetic operations [Tko18]; and even after such a pre-processing step, őļūĚúÖ\kappa can still be as large as dūĚĎĎd in the worst-case. Our algorithm has the benefit of not requiring any pre-processing step.

A third type of algorithms avoid projections by outputting Newton iterates that are guaranteed to be feasible thanks to the use of a self-concordant barrier. The first such algorithm in the context of online learning was introduced by [AHR09]. They presented a general recipe for using self-concordant barriers with Newton steps in online linear optimization. However, their approach falls short of being computationally-efficient as their algorithm needs to compute the inverse of the Hessian of the barrier at every iteration. Inspired by the work of [AHR09], [MR22a] used damped Newton steps with quadratic terms added to the barrier to design an efficient algorithm for the classical portfolio selection problem. Closer to our work is that of [MG22] who used a similar barrier for designing an algorithm for exp-concave optimization that can be viewed as a computationally-efficient version of the Online Newton Step [HAK07]. Similar to our work, [MG22] also leverage the stability of the Newton iterates to avoid computing the inverse of the Hessian of the barrier at every step. However, their approach and analysis, which are tailored to the exp-concave setting do not necessarily lead to improved regret bounds in the general OCO setting we consider. In particular, their algorithm does not lead to a O(T)fragmentsO(ūĚĎá)O(\sqrt{T}) regret bound over polytopes.

Finally, for our application to polytopes, we make use of recent tools and techniques developed for solving linear programs efficiently. In particular, we make use of the Lee-Sidford barrier [LS14, LS15, LS19], which can be computed efficiently and, when used to compute Newton iterates, leads to the state-of-the-art O~(d)fragments~ūĚĎā(ūĚĎĎ)\widetilde{O}(\sqrt{d}) iteration upper-bound for solving a linear program. For the OCO setting, we show that using the Lee-Sidford barrier within our algorithm leads to a O~(T)fragments~ūĚĎā(ūĚĎá)\widetilde{O}(\sqrt{T}) regret bound. We also note that ideas similar to the ones we use to avoid computing the inverse of the Hessian of the barrier at every round were used to amortize computations in the context of solving linear programs (see e.g.¬†[CLS21, vdB20, vdBLSS20]).

In section 2, we present our notation and relevant definitions. In Section 3, we present our algorithm and guarantees. In Section 4, we apply our results to the case of a polytope. All the proof are differed to the appendix.

Throughout the paper, we let ūĚí¶ūĚí¶\mathcal{K} be a closed convex subset of ‚ĄĚdfragmentsRūĚĎĎ\mathbb{R}^{d}. We denote by ‚ą•‚čÖ‚ą•fragments‚ą•‚čÖ‚ą•\|\cdot\| the Euclidean norm and by ‚Ą¨(R)‚äā‚ĄĚdfragmentsB(R)RūĚĎĎ\mathcal{B}(R)\subset\mathbb{R}^{d} the Euclidean ball of radius R>0fragmentsR0R>0. We let intūĚí¶fragmentsintK\operatorname{int}\mathcal{K} denote the interior of ūĚí¶ūĚí¶\mathcal{K}.

Our main algorithm, which can be viewed as an ‚Äúonline‚ÄĚ counter-part to the Newton iterations [N+{}^{+}18], uses a self-concordant barrier over the set of interest to avoid the need of performing Euclidean projections onto ūĚí¶ūĚí¶\mathcal{K}. Next, we present the definition of a self-concordant barrier.

For the rest of this section, we let ūĚí¶ūĚí¶\mathcal{K} be a convex compact set with non-empty interior intūĚí¶fragmentsintK\operatorname{int}\mathcal{K}. Among existing OCO algorithms that achieve a ~O(T) regret bound, none can achieve this computational complexity for general polytopes with m constraints (see Section 4 for more details).3.1subsection¬†3.13.1¬ß3.13.1Efficient Computation of the Newton Iterates with BARONSThe key feature of BARONS (Algorithm 1) is that is uses an amortized computation of the Hessians. Namely, BARONS computes the inverse of the Hessian of the barrier ő¶ only for a small fractions of the iterates (wt). Henceforth, we refer to the iterates where the algorithm computes the full inverse of the Hessian as landmark iterates; these are the iterates (ut) in Lines 13 and 16 of Algorithm 1. The idea behind this is that for a sufficiently curved3footnote¬†33footnote 3Informally, the ‚Äúcurvature‚ÄĚ of a convex function is high when the rate of change of its gradients is high. barrier ő¶, the Newton iterates with respect to ő¶ are stable enough that it suffices to compute the inverse of the Hessian of ő¶ at the closest landmark iterate. For example, this is what was done in [MG22] to design an efficient algorithm for exp-concave optimization.Unlike the setting of [MG22], where it is possible to add quadratic terms to the barrier for additional stability, in our setting we cannot do that without sacrificing performance in terms of regret. Without the quadratic terms, the Newton iterates are not stable enough for our desired guarantee. Instead of adding regularization terms, BARONS takes ~O(1) Newton steps per round to get ‚Äúcloser‚ÄĚ to the Newton iterate with the true Hessian matrix. This simple approach is key to the success of our approach.In the next subsection, we give a generic guarantee for BARONS.Algorithm 111Algorithm 11BARONS: Barrier-Regularized Online Newton StepAlgorithm 1 BARONS: Barrier-Regularized Online Newton Step0:00¬†¬†¬†‚ÄĘitem¬†1st itemParameters ő∑,őĶ,őĪ>0, and mNewton‚Č•1.‚ÄĘitem¬†2nd itemGradient/Hessian Oracles OgradőĶ/OhessőĪ for self-concordant function ő¶ with constant Mő¶>0.‚ÄĘitem¬†3rd itemw‚čÜ‚ąąargminw‚ąąKő¶(w).1:11¬†¬†Set u1=w1=w‚čÜ, H1=OhessőĪ(w‚čÜ), and s0=0.2:22¬†¬†for¬†t=1,‚Ä¶,T¬†do3:33¬†¬†¬†¬†¬†Play wt and observe gt‚ąą‚ąā‚Ąďt(wt).4:44¬†¬†¬†¬†¬† Set st=st-1+ő∑gt.5:55¬†¬†¬†¬†¬†Set wt+11=wt.5:55¬†¬†¬†¬†¬†  //Perform intermediate Newton steps to decrease the Newton decrement6:66¬†¬†¬†¬†¬†for¬†m=1,‚Ä¶,mNewton-1¬†do7:77¬†¬†¬†¬†¬†¬†¬†¬†Set ^‚ąát+1m=OgradőĶ(wt+1m).8:88¬†¬†¬†¬†¬†¬†¬†¬† Set ~‚ąát+1m=^‚ąát+1m+st. //~‚ąát+1m‚Čą‚ąáő¶t+1(wt+1m)9:99¬†¬†¬†¬†¬†¬†¬†¬†Set wt+1m+1=wt+1m-Ht-1~‚ąát+1m.10:1010¬†¬†¬†¬†¬†end¬†for11:1111¬†¬†¬†¬†¬†Set wt+1=wt+1m+1.11:1111¬†¬†¬†¬†¬†  //Check if the landmark needs updating12:1212¬†¬†¬†¬†¬†if¬†‚ą•wt+1-ut‚ą•Ht‚Č§1/(41Mő¶)¬†then 13:1313¬†¬†¬†¬†¬†¬†¬†¬†Set ut+1=ut.  //Update landmark 14:1414¬†¬†¬†¬†¬†¬†¬†¬†Ht+1=Ht and H-1t+1=H-1t.15:1515¬†¬†¬†¬†¬†else16:1616¬†¬†¬†¬†¬†¬†¬†¬†Set ut+1=wt+1.  //No landmark update 17:1717¬†¬†¬†¬†¬†¬†¬†¬†Set Ht+1=OhessőĪ(ut+1) and compute H-1t+1. 18:1818¬†¬†¬†¬†¬†end¬†if19:1919¬†¬†end¬†for3.2subsection¬†3.23.2¬ß3.23.2Generic Regret Guarantee of BARONSIn this subsection, we present a general regret and computational guarantee for BARONS under minimal assumptions on the sequence of losses and without turning the ‚Äústep size‚ÄĚ ő∑. This will then allow us to instantiate the guarantees of BARONS in Section 3 and provide explicit and state-of-the-art bounds on the regret of BARONS.We will assume without loss of generality that ‚ą•ai‚ą•=1, for all i‚ąą[m], and let A‚ČĒ(a1,‚Ä¶,am)\top‚ąąRm√ód denote the constraint matrix of the set K.For the rest of this section, it will be convenient to define the ‚Äúslack‚ÄĚ variables sw,i=ai\topw-b‚Ä≤i, for i‚ąą[m]. Here, sw,i essentially represents the distance of w to the ith facet of the polytope K. Further, we let Sw‚ČĒdiag(sw) be the diagonal matrix whose ith diagonal entry is sw,i.The LS barrier.To perform Online Convex Optimization over K,we pick the regularizer ő¶ of BARONS to be the Lee-Sidford (LS) barrier ő¶LS¬†[LS19] with parameter p>0, which is defined aső¶LS(v)=minv‚ąąRm>0-logdet(A\topSwVSwA)+11+p-1tr(V1+1/p),ő¶LS(v)=minv‚ąąRm>0-logdet(A\topSwVSwA)+11+p-1tr(V1+1/p),where V=diag(v). One way to think of the LS barrier is as a weighted log-barrier. As we will discuss in the sequel, this choice will confer computational and performance (in terms of regret) advantages over the standard log-barrier.Self-concordance of the LS barrier.According to¬†[FLPS22, Theorem 30], the LS barrier with the choice p=O(log(m)) is a self-concordant function with parameter Mő¶LS satisfyingMő¶LS=O(log(m)2/5)=~O(1),Mő¶LS=O(log(m)2/5)=~O(1),The other favorable property of this barrier is that its Newton decrement at any point w‚ąąK is of order ~O(d); that is,(11)Equation¬†1111‚ą•‚ąáő¶LS(w)‚ą•‚ąá-2ő¶LS(w)=~O(d).‚ą•‚ąáő¶LS(w)‚ą•‚ąá-2ő¶LS(w)=~O(d).Therefore, ő¶LS is a (~O(1),~O(d))-self-concordant barrier. For the log-barrier, the right-hand side of (11) would be m.Cost of gradient and Hessian Oracles.We consider the computational complexities of gradient and Hessian Oracles for ő¶LS. By [LS19], we have that for őĶ>0,CgradőĶ(ő¶LS)‚Č§~O(Csys‚čÖlog(1/őĶ)),andChessőĶ(ő¶LS)‚Č§~O(Csysd‚čÖlog(1/őĶ)),CgradőĶ(ő¶LS)‚Č§~O(Csys‚čÖlog(1/őĶ)),andChessőĶ(ő¶LS)‚Č§~O(Csysd‚čÖlog(1/őĶ)),where Csys is the computational cost of solving a linear system of the form A\topdiag(v)Ax=y, for vectors v‚ąąRd‚Č•0 and y‚ąąRd; we recall that A=(a1,‚Ä¶,am)\top is the constraint matrix for K. In the worst-case, such a linear system can be solved with cost bounded as(12)Equation¬†1212Csys‚Č§O(mdŌČ-1),Csys‚Č§O(mdŌČ-1),where ŌČ is the exponent of matrix multiplication, and m is the number of constraints of K. However, as we show in the sequel, Csys can be much smaller in many practical applications.With this, we immediately obtain the following corollary for the regret and run-time of BARONS under local norm and Euclidean norm bounds on the sub-gradients.Corollary 111Corollary 1Corollary 1 (OCO over a polytope with LS barrier).Let c‚ąą(0,1),G,R,b>0, and suppose K is given by (10) and that ő¶LS is the corresponding LS barrier. Using the LS barrier replaces the m term in this bound by d regardless of the number of constraints‚ÄĒsee (13). However, this comes at a Csys computational cost, which can be as high as mdŌČ-1 in the worst-case (see (12)). In the case of the log-barrier, this cost would be replaced by md (essentially because CgradőĶ(ő¶log)‚Č§O(md)). Thus, when m is of the order of d, using the log-barrier may be more computational-efficient compared to using the LS barrier. In contrast, the best dimension-free regret bound4footnote¬†44footnote 4The dependence in T can be improved under additional structure such as smoothness or strong-convexity of the losses. achieved by existing projection-free algorithms is of order O(T3/4) (see e.g.¬†[HK12, GK22]). We also note that existing separation/membership-based algorithms that achieve a T regret; for examples those presented in [Mha22a, GK22, LBGH23], are not dimension-free. Their regret bounds are of order O(őļT), where őļ=R/r with r,R>0 such that B(r)‚äÜK‚äÜB(R). The asphercity parameter can depend on the dimension d [Mha22a], and even after a pre-conditioning step (which would involve putting the set K into near-isotropic position and can cost up to ő©(d4) [LV06]), őļ can be as large as d in the worst-case. Of course, to make a fair comparison with existing projection-free algorithms, we also need to take computational complexity into account. This is what we do next.Computational cost.The computational cost in (15) should be compared with that of existing projection-free algorithms. For linear optimization-based projection-free algorithms, the computational cost after T rounds is typically of order Clin‚čÖT, where Clin is the cost of performing linear optimization over K which, for a polytope K, reduces to solving a linear program. Using state-of-the-art interior point methods for solving such a linear program would cost Clin‚Č§~O(d‚čÖCsys); see e.g.¬†[LS19]. Thus, linear optimization-based projection-free algorithms5footnote¬†55footnote 5This only concerns algorithms that use an interior point method to implement linear optimization over K. can have a cost that is a factor d worse than that of BARONS in the setting of Corollary 1. On the other hand, separation/membership-based algorithms, the computational cost scales with O(Csep‚čÖT) after T rounds, where Csep is the cost of performing separation for the set K. For a general polytope in Rd with m constraints, we have Csep‚Č§O(md), which may be smaller than Csys (the latter can be as large as mdŌČ-1 in the worse case;see (12)). Here, it may be more appropriate to compare against the computational guarantee of BARONS given in Corollary 2; by (18), we have that for T‚Č•dŌČ-2m, the computational cost of BARONS in the setting of the corollary is dominated by (md+d2)‚čÖT, which is comparable to that of existing separation-based algorithms.4.2subsection¬†4.24.2¬ß4.24.2Implications for Non-Lipschitz LossesAnother advantage BARONS has over projection-free, and even projection-based, algorithms is that it has a regret bound that scales with a bound on the local norms of the gradients‚ÄĒsee (13). We now showcase two online learning settings where this leads to non-trivial performance and computational improvements over existing OCO algorithms.Online Portfolio Selection [Cov91].The portfolio selection problem is a classical online learning problem where the gradients of the losses can be unbounded. In this paragraph, we demonstrate how the guarantee of BARONS in Corollary 1 leads to a non-trivial guarantee for this setting both in terms of regret and computational complexity. In the online portfolio setting, at each round t, a learner (algorithm) chooses a distribution wt‚ąąőĒd over a fixed set of d portfolios. Then, the environment reveals a return vector rt‚ąąR‚Č•0d, and the learner suffers a loss‚Ąďt(wt)‚ČĒ-logwt\toprt.The goal of the learner is to minimize the regret RegT(w)‚ČĒ‚ąĎt=1T(‚Ąďt(wt)-‚Ąďt(w)) after T‚Č•1 rounds. For this problem, it is known that a logarithmic regret is achievable, but the specialized algorithms that achieve this have a computational complexity that scales with min(d3T,d2T2) [Cov91, LWZ18, MR22b, ZAK22, JOG22]. On the other hand, applying the generic Online Gradient Descent or the Online Newton Step to this problem leads to regret bounds that scale with the maximum norm of the gradient (which can be unbounded). Instantiating the guarantees of BARONS in Corollary 1 with ő¶ set to the standard log-barrier for the simplex6footnote¬†66footnote 6Technically, we need to use a barrier for the set {~w‚ąąRd‚Č•0‚ą£‚ąĎi‚ąą[d-1]~wi‚Č§1}; see e.g.¬†[MR22b]., in particular the bound in (13), to the online portfolio selection problem leads to an ~O(dT) regret bound, which does not depend on the norm of the observed gradients. Furthermore, we have Csys‚Č§O(d), and so by (15) the computational complexity is essentially O(d2T) after T rounds. Technically, the bound in (13) is only against comparators in the restricted set Kc. However, by setting c=1/T, it possible to extend this guarantee to all comparators in intK as explained in Remark 1, since the losses in this case satisfy (6) [LWZ18, Lemma 10].Linear prediction with the log-loss.Another classical online learning problem with unbounded gradients is that of linear prediction with the log-loss [RS15]. For this problem, at each round t, the learner receives a feature vector xt‚ąąX‚äÜRd, outputs wt‚ąąW‚äÜRd, then observes label yt‚ąąY‚ČĒ{-1,1} and suffers loss‚Ąďt(wt)‚ČĒ-I{yt=1}‚čÖlog(1-wt\topxt)-I{yt=0}‚čÖlog(1-wt\topxt).‚Ąďt(wt)‚ČĒ-I{yt=1}‚čÖlog(1-wt\topxt)-I{yt=0}‚čÖlog(1-wt\topxt).In the settings, where (X,W)=(őĒd,B‚ąě(1)) and (X,W)=(B‚ąě(1),őĒd), we have that ‚ą•‚ąá‚Ąďt(w)‚ą•‚ąá-2ő¶(w)‚Č§O(1) for all w‚ąąintW, where ő¶ is set to the corresponding log-barrier for W. Thus, instantiating Corollary 1 (in particular (13)) in this setting implies that BARONS achieves a regret bound of the form:(19)Equation¬†1919~O(dT),~O(dT),and has computational complexity bounded by ~O(d2T), as long as T‚Č•d. Again, we emphasize that the bound in (19) does not depend on the norm of the gradients, which may be unbounded.Finally, we note that there exist a few specialized algorithms that provide sublinear regret bounds for non-lipschitz losses. This includes, for example, the Soft-Bayes algorithm [OLL17]. However, this algorithm is specialized to the log-loss with a particular dependence on the predictions, and it is not clear, for example, what regret bound it would have in the linear prediction setting and other similar settings with non-Lipschitz losses.References[AHR09]AHR09AHR09Jacob¬†D Abernethy, Elad Hazan, and Alexander Rakhlin.Competing in the dark: An efficient algorithm for bandit linearoptimization.2009.[ALLW18]ALLW18ALLW18Jacob Abernethy, Kevin¬†A Lai, Kfir¬†Y Levy, and Jun-Kun Wang.Faster rates for convex-concave games.In Conference On Learning Theory, pages 1595‚Äď1625. The algorithm in question (Alg.¬†1) outputs approximate Newton iterates with respect to ‚Äúpotential functions‚ÄĚ (ő¶t) that take the following form:ő¶t(w)‚ČĒő¶(w)+w\top‚ąĎs=1t-1gs,ő¶t(w)‚ČĒő¶(w)+w\top‚ąĎs=1t-1gs,where (gs‚ąą‚ąā‚Ąďs(ws)) are the sub-gradients of the losses (‚Ąďs) at the iterates (ws) of Algorithm 1, and ő¶ is a self-concordant function over K. Algorithm¬†1 uses the the approximate gradient and Hessian Oracles of ő¶ (see 2) to output iterates (wt) approximate Newton iterates in the following sense:(1)Equation¬†11‚ąÄt‚ąą[T],wt+1‚Čąwt-‚ąá-2ő¶t+1(wt)‚ąáő¶t+1(wt).‚ąÄt‚ąą[T],wt+1‚Čąwt-‚ąá-2ő¶t+1(wt)‚ąáő¶t+1(wt).As is by now somewhat standard in the analyses of online Newton iterates of the form in (1), we will bound the regret of Algorithm 1 by showing that:‚ÄĘitem¬†1st itemThe iterates (wt) are close (in the norm induced by the Hessian ‚ąá2ő¶(wt)) to the FTRL iterates, which are given by(2)Equation¬†22wt‚čÜ‚ąąargminw‚ąąKő¶t(w).wt‚čÜ‚ąąargminw‚ąąKő¶t(w).‚ÄĘitem¬†2nd itemThe regret of FTRL is bounded by O(T).Our main contribution is an algorithm that outputs iterates (wt) that satisfy the first bullet point (i.e.¬†iterates that satisfy (1)) while only calling a Hessian Oracle (which is potentially computationally expensive) a O(1/T) fraction of the rounds after T rounds. As we show in Section 4, for the case where K is a polytope with m‚ąąN constraints, the algorithm achieves a ~O(T) regret bound, where the per-iteration computational cost essentially reduces to a linear-system-solve involving a d√óm matrix. Among existing OCO algorithms that achieve a ~O(T) regret bound, none can achieve this computational complexity for general polytopes with m constraints (see Section 4 for more details).3.1subsection¬†3.13.1¬ß3.13.1Efficient Computation of the Newton Iterates with BARONSThe key feature of BARONS (Algorithm 1) is that is uses an amortized computation of the Hessians. Namely, BARONS computes the inverse of the Hessian of the barrier ő¶ only for a small fractions of the iterates (wt). Henceforth, we refer to the iterates where the algorithm computes the full inverse of the Hessian as landmark iterates; these are the iterates (ut) in Lines 13 and 16 of Algorithm 1. The idea behind this is that for a sufficiently curved3footnote¬†33footnote 3Informally, the ‚Äúcurvature‚ÄĚ of a convex function is high when the rate of change of its gradients is high. barrier ő¶, the Newton iterates with respect to ő¶ are stable enough that it suffices to compute the inverse of the Hessian of ő¶ at the closest landmark iterate. For example, this is what was done in [MG22] to design an efficient algorithm for exp-concave optimization.Unlike the setting of [MG22], where it is possible to add quadratic terms to the barrier for additional stability, in our setting we cannot do that without sacrificing performance in terms of regret. Without the quadratic terms, the Newton iterates are not stable enough for our desired guarantee. Instead of adding regularization terms, BARONS takes ~O(1) Newton steps per round to get ‚Äúcloser‚ÄĚ to the Newton iterate with the true Hessian matrix. This simple approach is key to the success of our approach.In the next subsection, we give a generic guarantee for BARONS.Algorithm 111Algorithm 11BARONS: Barrier-Regularized Online Newton StepAlgorithm 1 BARONS: Barrier-Regularized Online Newton Step0:00¬†¬†¬†‚ÄĘitem¬†1st itemParameters ő∑,őĶ,őĪ>0, and mNewton‚Č•1.‚ÄĘitem¬†2nd itemGradient/Hessian Oracles OgradőĶ/OhessőĪ for self-concordant function ő¶ with constant Mő¶>0.‚ÄĘitem¬†3rd itemw‚čÜ‚ąąargminw‚ąąKő¶(w).1:11¬†¬†Set u1=w1=w‚čÜ, H1=OhessőĪ(w‚čÜ), and s0=0.2:22¬†¬†for¬†t=1,‚Ä¶,T¬†do3:33¬†¬†¬†¬†¬†Play wt and observe gt‚ąą‚ąā‚Ąďt(wt).4:44¬†¬†¬†¬†¬† Set st=st-1+ő∑gt.5:55¬†¬†¬†¬†¬†Set wt+11=wt.5:55¬†¬†¬†¬†¬†  //Perform intermediate Newton steps to decrease the Newton decrement6:66¬†¬†¬†¬†¬†for¬†m=1,‚Ä¶,mNewton-1¬†do7:77¬†¬†¬†¬†¬†¬†¬†¬†Set ^‚ąát+1m=OgradőĶ(wt+1m).8:88¬†¬†¬†¬†¬†¬†¬†¬† Set ~‚ąát+1m=^‚ąát+1m+st. //~‚ąát+1m‚Čą‚ąáő¶t+1(wt+1m)9:99¬†¬†¬†¬†¬†¬†¬†¬†Set wt+1m+1=wt+1m-Ht-1~‚ąát+1m.10:1010¬†¬†¬†¬†¬†end¬†for11:1111¬†¬†¬†¬†¬†Set wt+1=wt+1m+1.11:1111¬†¬†¬†¬†¬†  //Check if the landmark needs updating12:1212¬†¬†¬†¬†¬†if¬†‚ą•wt+1-ut‚ą•Ht‚Č§1/(41Mő¶)¬†then 13:1313¬†¬†¬†¬†¬†¬†¬†¬†Set ut+1=ut.  //Update landmark 14:1414¬†¬†¬†¬†¬†¬†¬†¬†Ht+1=Ht and H-1t+1=H-1t.15:1515¬†¬†¬†¬†¬†else16:1616¬†¬†¬†¬†¬†¬†¬†¬†Set ut+1=wt+1.  //No landmark update 17:1717¬†¬†¬†¬†¬†¬†¬†¬†Set Ht+1=OhessőĪ(ut+1) and compute H-1t+1. 18:1818¬†¬†¬†¬†¬†end¬†if19:1919¬†¬†end¬†for3.2subsection¬†3.23.2¬ß3.23.2Generic Regret Guarantee of BARONSIn this subsection, we present a general regret and computational guarantee for BARONS under minimal assumptions on the sequence of losses and without turning the ‚Äústep size‚ÄĚ ő∑. This will then allow us to instantiate the guarantees of BARONS in Section 3 and provide explicit and state-of-the-art bounds on the regret of BARONS.We will assume without loss of generality that ‚ą•ai‚ą•=1, for all i‚ąą[m], and let A‚ČĒ(a1,‚Ä¶,am)\top‚ąąRm√ód denote the constraint matrix of the set K.For the rest of this section, it will be convenient to define the ‚Äúslack‚ÄĚ variables sw,i=ai\topw-b‚Ä≤i, for i‚ąą[m]. Here, sw,i essentially represents the distance of w to the ith facet of the polytope K. Further, we let Sw‚ČĒdiag(sw) be the diagonal matrix whose ith diagonal entry is sw,i.The LS barrier.To perform Online Convex Optimization over K,we pick the regularizer ő¶ of BARONS to be the Lee-Sidford (LS) barrier ő¶LS¬†[LS19] with parameter p>0, which is defined aső¶LS(v)=minv‚ąąRm>0-logdet(A\topSwVSwA)+11+p-1tr(V1+1/p),ő¶LS(v)=minv‚ąąRm>0-logdet(A\topSwVSwA)+11+p-1tr(V1+1/p),where V=diag(v). One way to think of the LS barrier is as a weighted log-barrier. As we will discuss in the sequel, this choice will confer computational and performance (in terms of regret) advantages over the standard log-barrier.Self-concordance of the LS barrier.According to¬†[FLPS22, Theorem 30], the LS barrier with the choice p=O(log(m)) is a self-concordant function with parameter Mő¶LS satisfyingMő¶LS=O(log(m)2/5)=~O(1),Mő¶LS=O(log(m)2/5)=~O(1),The other favorable property of this barrier is that its Newton decrement at any point w‚ąąK is of order ~O(d); that is,(11)Equation¬†1111‚ą•‚ąáő¶LS(w)‚ą•‚ąá-2ő¶LS(w)=~O(d).‚ą•‚ąáő¶LS(w)‚ą•‚ąá-2ő¶LS(w)=~O(d).Therefore, ő¶LS is a (~O(1),~O(d))-self-concordant barrier. For the log-barrier, the right-hand side of (11) would be m.Cost of gradient and Hessian Oracles.We consider the computational complexities of gradient and Hessian Oracles for ő¶LS. By [LS19], we have that for őĶ>0,CgradőĶ(ő¶LS)‚Č§~O(Csys‚čÖlog(1/őĶ)),andChessőĶ(ő¶LS)‚Č§~O(Csysd‚čÖlog(1/őĶ)),CgradőĶ(ő¶LS)‚Č§~O(Csys‚čÖlog(1/őĶ)),andChessőĶ(ő¶LS)‚Č§~O(Csysd‚čÖlog(1/őĶ)),where Csys is the computational cost of solving a linear system of the form A\topdiag(v)Ax=y, for vectors v‚ąąRd‚Č•0 and y‚ąąRd; we recall that A=(a1,‚Ä¶,am)\top is the constraint matrix for K. In the worst-case, such a linear system can be solved with cost bounded as(12)Equation¬†1212Csys‚Č§O(mdŌČ-1),Csys‚Č§O(mdŌČ-1),where ŌČ is the exponent of matrix multiplication, and m is the number of constraints of K. However, as we show in the sequel, Csys can be much smaller in many practical applications.With this, we immediately obtain the following corollary for the regret and run-time of BARONS under local norm and Euclidean norm bounds on the sub-gradients.Corollary 111Corollary 1Corollary 1 (OCO over a polytope with LS barrier).Let c‚ąą(0,1),G,R,b>0, and suppose K is given by (10) and that ő¶LS is the corresponding LS barrier. Further, let (wt) be the iterates of BARONS, and let Kc be the restricted version of K defined in (4). Then, the following holds:‚ÄĘitem¬†1st itemLocal norm bound: If ‚ą•gt‚ą•‚ąá-2ő¶(wt)‚Č§b, for all t‚Č•1, and the parameters (ő∑,őĶ,őĪ,mNewton) of BARONS are set as in Theorem¬†6 with ő¶=ő¶LS and (Mő¶,őĹ)=(~O(1),~O(d)), then for T large enough (as specified in Theorem 6), the regret of BARONS is bounded by(13)Equation¬†1313RegBARONST(w)\lesssimbdTlogc,‚ąÄw‚ąąKc.RegBARONST(w)\lesssimbdTlogc,‚ąÄw‚ąąKc.‚ÄĘitem¬†2nd itemEuclidean norm bound: If K‚äÜB(R) and ‚ą•gt‚ą•‚Č§G, for all t‚Č•1, and the parameters (ő∑,őĶ,őĪ,mNewton) of BARONS are set as in Theorem 7 with ő¶(‚čÖ)=ő¶LS(‚čÖ)+őĹ2R2‚ą•‚čÖ‚ą•2 and (Mő®,őĹ)=(~O(1),~O(d)), then for T large enough (as in Theorem 7) BARONS has regret bounded as(14)Equation¬†1414RegBARONST(w)\lesssimRGTlogT,‚ąÄw‚ąąintK.RegBARONST(w)\lesssimRGTlogT,‚ąÄw‚ąąintK.In either case, the computational complexity is bounded by(15)Equation¬†1515~O((Csys+d2)‚čÖT+Csys‚čÖdT),~O((Csys+d2)‚čÖT+Csys‚čÖdT),where Csys is the computational cost of solving a linear system of the form A\topdiag(v)Ax=y, for vectors v‚ąąRd‚Č•0 and y‚ąąRd (recall that A is the constraint matrix for the polytope K).Using the log-barrier.We note that since K is a polytope, we could have used the standard log-barrier(16)Equation¬†1616ő¶log(w)‚ČĒ‚ąĎi=1mlog(bi‚Ä≤-ai\topw).ő¶log(w)‚ČĒ‚ąĎi=1mlog(bi‚Ä≤-ai\topw).This barrier is (1,m)-self-concordant, and so instantiating Theorem 5 with it would imply a ~O(bmdT) regret bound in the case of local sub-gradient norms bounded by b>0. Using the LS barrier replaces the m term in this bound by d regardless of the number of constraints‚ÄĒsee (13). However, this comes at a Csys computational cost, which can be as high as mdŌČ-1 in the worst-case (see (12)). In the case of the log-barrier, this cost would be replaced by md (essentially because CgradőĶ(ő¶log)‚Č§O(md)). Thus, when m is of the order of d, using the log-barrier may be more computational-efficient compared to using the LS barrier. In the next corollary, we bound the regret of BARONS when ő¶=ő¶log; this result is an immediate consequence of Theorem 7.Corollary 222Corollary 2Corollary 2 (OCO over a polytope with the log barrier).Let G,b>0, and suppose K is given by (10) and that ő¶log is the corresponding log-barrier. Further, let (wt) be the iterates of BARONS. If K‚äÜB(R) and ‚ą•gt‚ą•‚Č§G, for all t‚Č•1, and the parameters (ő∑,őĶ,őĪ,mNewton) of BARONS are set as in Theorem 7 with ő¶(‚čÖ)=ő¶log(‚čÖ)+őĹ2R2‚ą•‚čÖ‚ą•2 and (Mő®,őĹ)=(1,m), then for T large enough (as in Theorem 7) BARONS has regret bounded as(17)Equation¬†1717RegBARONST(w)\lesssimRGTlogT,‚ąÄw‚ąąintK.RegBARONST(w)\lesssimRGTlogT,‚ąÄw‚ąąintK.The computational complexity is bounded by(18)Equation¬†1818~O((md+d2)‚čÖT+mdŌČ-1mT).~O((md+d2)‚čÖT+mdŌČ-1mT).4.1subsection¬†4.14.1¬ß4.14.1Implications for Lipschitz LossesWe now discuss implications of Corollary 1, and compare the bound of BARONS to those of existing algorithms for Lipschitz losses.Dimension-free regret bound.We note when the Euclidean norms of the sub-gradients are bounded, BARONS achieves a dimension-free O(T) regret bound. In contrast, the best dimension-free regret bound4footnote¬†44footnote 4The dependence in T can be improved under additional structure such as smoothness or strong-convexity of the losses. achieved by existing projection-free algorithms is of order O(T3/4) (see e.g.¬†[HK12, GK22]). We also note that existing separation/membership-based algorithms that achieve a T regret; for examples those presented in [Mha22a, GK22, LBGH23], are not dimension-free. Their regret bounds are of order O(őļT), where őļ=R/r with r,R>0 such that B(r)‚äÜK‚äÜB(R). The asphercity parameter can depend on the dimension d [Mha22a], and even after a pre-conditioning step (which would involve putting the set K into near-isotropic position and can cost up to ő©(d4) [LV06]), őļ can be as large as d in the worst-case. Of course, to make a fair comparison with existing projection-free algorithms, we also need to take computational complexity into account. This is what we do next.Computational cost.The computational cost in (15) should be compared with that of existing projection-free algorithms. For linear optimization-based projection-free algorithms, the computational cost after T rounds is typically of order Clin‚čÖT, where Clin is the cost of performing linear optimization over K which, for a polytope K, reduces to solving a linear program. Using state-of-the-art interior point methods for solving such a linear program would cost Clin‚Č§~O(d‚čÖCsys); see e.g.¬†[LS19]. Thus, linear optimization-based projection-free algorithms5footnote¬†55footnote 5This only concerns algorithms that use an interior point method to implement linear optimization over K. can have a cost that is a factor d worse than that of BARONS in the setting of Corollary 1. On the other hand, separation/membership-based algorithms, the computational cost scales with O(Csep‚čÖT) after T rounds, where Csep is the cost of performing separation for the set K. For a general polytope in Rd with m constraints, we have Csep‚Č§O(md), which may be smaller than Csys (the latter can be as large as mdŌČ-1 in the worse case;see (12)). Here, it may be more appropriate to compare against the computational guarantee of BARONS given in Corollary 2; by (18), we have that for T‚Č•dŌČ-2m, the computational cost of BARONS in the setting of the corollary is dominated by (md+d2)‚čÖT, which is comparable to that of existing separation-based algorithms.4.2subsection¬†4.24.2¬ß4.24.2Implications for Non-Lipschitz LossesAnother advantage BARONS has over projection-free, and even projection-based, algorithms is that it has a regret bound that scales with a bound on the local norms of the gradients‚ÄĒsee (13). We now showcase two online learning settings where this leads to non-trivial performance and computational improvements over existing OCO algorithms.Online Portfolio Selection [Cov91].The portfolio selection problem is a classical online learning problem where the gradients of the losses can be unbounded. In this paragraph, we demonstrate how the guarantee of BARONS in Corollary 1 leads to a non-trivial guarantee for this setting both in terms of regret and computational complexity. In the online portfolio setting, at each round t, a learner (algorithm) chooses a distribution wt‚ąąőĒd over a fixed set of d portfolios. Then, the environment reveals a return vector rt‚ąąR‚Č•0d, and the learner suffers a loss‚Ąďt(wt)‚ČĒ-logwt\toprt.The goal of the learner is to minimize the regret RegT(w)‚ČĒ‚ąĎt=1T(‚Ąďt(wt)-‚Ąďt(w)) after T‚Č•1 rounds. For this problem, it is known that a logarithmic regret is achievable, but the specialized algorithms that achieve this have a computational complexity that scales with min(d3T,d2T2) [Cov91, LWZ18, MR22b, ZAK22, JOG22]. On the other hand, applying the generic Online Gradient Descent or the Online Newton Step to this problem leads to regret bounds that scale with the maximum norm of the gradient (which can be unbounded). Instantiating the guarantees of BARONS in Corollary 1 with ő¶ set to the standard log-barrier for the simplex6footnote¬†66footnote 6Technically, we need to use a barrier for the set {~w‚ąąRd‚Č•0‚ą£‚ąĎi‚ąą[d-1]~wi‚Č§1}; see e.g.¬†[MR22b]., in particular the bound in (13), to the online portfolio selection problem leads to an ~O(dT) regret bound, which does not depend on the norm of the observed gradients. Furthermore, we have Csys‚Č§O(d), and so by (15) the computational complexity is essentially O(d2T) after T rounds. Technically, the bound in (13) is only against comparators in the restricted set Kc. However, by setting c=1/T, it possible to extend this guarantee to all comparators in intK as explained in Remark 1, since the losses in this case satisfy (6) [LWZ18, Lemma 10].Linear prediction with the log-loss.Another classical online learning problem with unbounded gradients is that of linear prediction with the log-loss [RS15]. For this problem, at each round t, the learner receives a feature vector xt‚ąąX‚äÜRd, outputs wt‚ąąW‚äÜRd, then observes label yt‚ąąY‚ČĒ{-1,1} and suffers loss‚Ąďt(wt)‚ČĒ-I{yt=1}‚čÖlog(1-wt\topxt)-I{yt=0}‚čÖlog(1-wt\topxt).‚Ąďt(wt)‚ČĒ-I{yt=1}‚čÖlog(1-wt\topxt)-I{yt=0}‚čÖlog(1-wt\topxt).In the settings, where (X,W)=(őĒd,B‚ąě(1)) and (X,W)=(B‚ąě(1),őĒd), we have that ‚ą•‚ąá‚Ąďt(w)‚ą•‚ąá-2ő¶(w)‚Č§O(1) for all w‚ąąintW, where ő¶ is set to the corresponding log-barrier for W. Thus, instantiating Corollary 1 (in particular (13)) in this setting implies that BARONS achieves a regret bound of the form:(19)Equation¬†1919~O(dT),~O(dT),and has computational complexity bounded by ~O(d2T), as long as T‚Č•d. Again, we emphasize that the bound in (19) does not depend on the norm of the gradients, which may be unbounded.Finally, we note that there exist a few specialized algorithms that provide sublinear regret bounds for non-lipschitz losses. This includes, for example, the Soft-Bayes algorithm [OLL17]. However, this algorithm is specialized to the log-loss with a particular dependence on the predictions, and it is not clear, for example, what regret bound it would have in the linear prediction setting and other similar settings with non-Lipschitz losses.References[AHR09]AHR09AHR09Jacob¬†D Abernethy, Elad Hazan, and Alexander Rakhlin.Competing in the dark: An efficient algorithm for bandit linearoptimization.2009.[ALLW18]ALLW18ALLW18Jacob Abernethy, Kevin¬†A Lai, Kfir¬†Y Levy, and Jun-Kun Wang.Faster rates for convex-concave games.In Conference On Learning Theory, pages 1595‚Äď1625.
